Integrated beam-slab
Master thesis - DTU Byg
January - July 2016
Daniel D. Vidstrup - s112847
==PAGE:2==
==PAGE:3==
DTU - Byg
Forord
The project about integrated beam-slabs is made at the Technical University of Denmark
in the building and construction department. The main supervisor on the project has been
Kristian Herts, but also Morten Rasmussen from Abeo has helped.
The idea of the integrated beam-slab arises from the idea of a lower ﬂoor height. It the deck
elements can be connected on the construction site, the use of beams can be saved and the
ﬂoor height can be lowered.
The projects intention is to investigate the possibilities of connecting the deck elements on
the construction site and investigate the allowed lengths of the SL-decks. The applied load are
calculated as the Eurocodes require and the bearing capacity of the decks are determined using
Abeo’s Designer worksheet.
Though the project several unique persons have helped. These persons include Marianne and
Mia from DTU byg, who supervised about the casting of the connections. Other persons are
Casper from Zueblin, who shared experience about the meva form work.
Daniel D. Vidstrup
June 2016
I
==PAGE:4==
==PAGE:5==
DTU - Byg
Abstract
The integrated beam-slab is a new way to use the deck elements. By joining them together
on the construction site, the idea is that the beams can be saved and a lover ﬂoor hight is
achieved.
Through the project diﬀerent length and load combinations have been investigated, both for
the SL-deck and the beam-slab. With a deck height of 320 mm a SL-length of up to 15 m can
be reached when the SL-deck are ﬁxed to the beam-slabs. Otherwise the maximum length is a
bit shorter.
The length of the SL-deck create challenges for the dimensioning of the beam-slab. Especially
for the beam-slab in a 2-span structure, where most of the weight is taken by the middle beam-
slap.
The shear forces in the beam-slab reaches 888 kN and the negative moment across
the beam-slab reached 1071 kNm. All calculations are highly conservative because an area
reduction coeﬃcient is neglected. Despite, the amount of forces in the middle beam-slab are
extremely high, and it is the conclusion that shorter spans have to be used to make this idea
possible.
Also the joint have problems with the bearing capacity. The 1-span structure creates positive
moment in the joint, which means a the joint have to be anchored in the bottom. In idea to
solve this have been presented but an actual solution have not been investigated.
In the end a few recommendations are made for the further work with the integrated beam-
slab concept.

DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab - Intro
1
Introduction
1.1
General introduction
Due to the fact that people still move into larger cities, engineers are facing a larger demand
of optimizing the living space in cities. Most cities do not have the possibility to grow horizontal
and therefore they have to grow vertically. Sometimes buildings are renovated and sometimes
the buildings are destroyed and new buildings are built.
When a new building is designed, engineers have to work within given regulations. Often
the municipality has regulations about the building height and how large percentage of the
land registry that may be used. These regulations set the engineers in a diﬃcult situation.
The investors would like as much ﬂoor area as possible but the engineers can not build as high
as they want or have budget for. Therefore, engineers want a ﬂoor height as low as possible.
Also, many companies want to have oﬃce building with large open spaces. Normally oﬃces are
ﬁlled with columns and walls to carry the weight, so employs face limitations when the oﬃce
is organized.
Normally, higher buildings are built by three components: columns, beams, and decks. These
three put on top of each other give the total ﬂoor height. By removing one of these components
the total ﬂoor height would be reduced and engineers could deliver more ﬂoors within the given
regulation about the building height.
The Danish Abeo concept has the possibility to deliver a solution for these problems. By
removing the beams, all decks have to absorb momentary forces and act like a plate together.
Abeo delivers super light decks (SL-deck) produced in a modern concrete factory. By removing
the light concrete of the deck, a high strength deck can be produced to absorb larger forces.
By combining these decks with and without light concrete and anchor them together at the
construction site, the plate mechanism can be achieved. The two types of decks could provide
a lower ﬂoor height with larger spans, so oﬃces can have large space instead of small limited
areas surrounded by columns and walls.
Since this concept is new for Abeo, the ﬁeld has not been investigated. Therefore, a solution
for the connections is highly relevant, since this seems to be an essential part of the design.
Section 1
Page 1 of 38
==PAGE:10==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab - Project description
1.2
Project description
The combination of light concrete decks, and high strength concrete decks acting like a beam
are new. The maximum length of the decks are highly relevant. Therefore, the following items
will be investigated:
• Determination of dimensioning moment and shear force for both slabs for diﬀerent lengths.
• Design of slabs and actual bearing capacity by using Abeo’s worksheet.
• Design of joints between slabs.
1.3
Limitations
In this project, it will be assumed that the solutions will be used in buildings designed for
oﬃce work. Therefore, load combinations will only contain oﬃce load given by the building
regulations. This is categorised as category B.
The dimensions of an oﬃce building can vary a lot, but often the building is long and slim.
Since the SL-decks are spanning along the slim length only two static systems are investigated
in this direction: one SL-deck and two SL-decks. The speciﬁc design is described in section 3.
The length of the beam-slab is only investigated for one setup, because this setup is investi-
gated for the diﬀerent lengths of SL-deck spanning across.
Another relevant parameter of the design is the height of the decks. In the following the
heigh of the decks will be set to 320 mm. In a further investigation this could be larger to
obtain higher bearing capacities.
Section 1
Page 2 of 38
==PAGE:11==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab - Method
2
Method
2.1
Procedure
First, the building regulations will be investigated and relevant sections regarding oﬃce build-
ings will be highlighted. This includes relevant loads and load combinations.
When the building regulations are clariﬁed, the calculations on the diﬀerent decks can begin.
The moment and shear force distribution for the SL-deck and beam-slab deck will be calculated
for diﬀerent spans and presented in tables.
Parallel to that, the actual bearing capacity of the decks will be determined using Abeo’s
Designer worksheet. This sheet is programmed to determine the moment and shear force bear-
ing capacity of the inhomogeneous decks.
When the relevant spans of the decks, and thereby the force distribution, are determined, the
connection between the SL-decks and beam-slabs will be investigated. This part will include
calculations on anchor length and suggested solutions for production of these joints.
Finally, the torsional force in the beam-slab will be investigated using simpliﬁed hand calcu-
lations and ﬁnite element modelling programs (FEM). These calculations are not included in
the Designer worksheet, since the decks originally not are designed to take these forces.
All the investigation above should lead to recommended spans of the SL-decks and beam-slab
decks and the joints between them.
2.2
Used theory
For calculations on the SL-deck the classic beam theory will be used - also known as Euler-
Bernouille’s beam theory. For calculations on the statically indeterminate beam-slab deck, the
force method will be used to determine reaction forces. Also the principle of superposition
might be used.
Both the classic beam theory, the force method, and principles of superposition are known
theory from the courses that DTU provides. The theories are used as it is described in the
book ’Statics and Mechanics of Structure’ [2] [3] of Krenk and Høgsberg.
Section 2
Page 3 of 38
==PAGE:12==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab
3
Design
In this section, the general design of the decks will brieﬂy be deﬁned, before the calculations
are made.
3.1
General
The combination of SL-decks and beam-slab decks opens an ocean of choices. Before the
calculations are started, some limitations have to be deﬁned. First, a sketch of the general
designs are shown in ﬁgure 3.1 below.
Figure 3.1: Sketch of the construction using beam-slab decks and SL-decks.
The ﬁgure shows that the SL-decks are spanning one span, from beam-slab to beam-slab.
Another model with 2 SL-decks in a row, connected by a beam-slab in the middle, are also
investigated. This is due to the fact that oﬃce buildings often are long and slim. Since the
SL-decks are spanning along the slim length of the building, the span will unlikely exceed two
SL-decks.
The idea is that the light SL-deck will be underpinned by the beam-slab deck, like it is
normally underpinned by a beam. The beam-slab will then be supported by columns or walls
which will bring the forces to the ground. Since the beam-slab can get too long to get trans-
ported from the factory to the construction site, the beam-slab is split into pieces and built up
following a Gerber construction principle. This is also shown in ﬁgure 3.1.
One of the general requirements to the decks is that it should ﬁt the concrete forms that
are used today.
This means that all decks should be 2400 mm wide and the pre-stressed
Section 3
Page 4 of 38
==PAGE:13==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab
reinforcement in the bottom can only be placed in existing spots. These placements are taken
from the Designer worksheet. Also the placement of the light concrete blocks should follow the
good practice used in the casting factory.
Another important variable is the hight of the decks. It is assumed that all decks should
have the same height, but another approach could be used. In some way, it could make sense
to make the decks heigher, where the forces are concentrated, but by opening this variable,
the amount of work could be almost inﬁnite. Therefore, it is decided that the height of all the
decks in this project is 320 mm.
3.2
SL-deck
The SL-deck is supposed to be as light as possible and span as long as possible. Therefore,
the SL-decks will be designed with 4 or 6 light concrete blocks across the width and with a
maximum amount of pre-stressed reinforcement in the bottom. An example of the cross-section
is shown in ﬁgure 3.2.
Figure 3.2: Cross section of the SL-deck
Furthermore, the SL-deck will be anchored to the beam-slab in order to transfer moment.
By doing this, the moment in the SL-deck is reduced and thereby a longer span is possible.
The SL-decks will not transfer any moment between each other.
3.3
Beam-slab deck
The beam-slab deck is supposed to transfer a high amount of forces from the SL-decks into
the columns or walls that support the beam-slab decks. Since the span of the SL-decks is
supposed to be as long as possible, the forces in the beam-slab decks are estimated to be high.
Therefore, the beam-slab decks will be designed with no light concrete blocks and relatively
short spans. The placement of the pre-stressed reinforcement is the same as in the SL-decks,
but by removing the light concrete blocks, the bearing capacity should be higher.
As mentioned earlier, the design of the beam-slab will take inspiration from a Gerber struc-
ture, where one deck is placed between two others. For this structure there will be moment
Section 3
Page 5 of 38
==PAGE:14==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab
transfer in the joints between the beam-slab decks. A traditionally Gerber structure has no
moment transfer.
3.4
Joints
The joints that connect the SL-decks to the beam-slab decks and the beam-slab decks between
are supposed to transfer shear and moment forces. In order to get the highest bearing capacity
in the joints, it is investigated, how the joints can be assembled on the construction site. Since
the joints contain anchors, the joints have to be cast. Instead of using a traditional joint, as
shown in ﬁgure 3.3, a joint cast on the construction site is investigated in order to optimize the
bearing capacity.
Figure 3.3: Example of a traditional joint between decks.
These joints should make the decks work like a plate and thereby the design with no beams
should be possible.

4
Theory of calculations
In this section, the theory behind the calculations will be presented. Most of the theory is
basic mechanical theory, which is assumed to be common knowledge and will therefore only be
brieﬂy presented.
4.1
General
The shear and moment force distributions are calculated following good engineering practice.
In the calculations, the shear and moment forces are separated into sections, in parts where
diﬀerent loads are applied.
4.2
Teknisk st˚abi
From Teknisk St˚abi [1] some of the simple force distributions to static indeterminate structures
are found. For an example, this is used when the reaction forces for the two span SL-deck are
determined.
4.3
Building regulations
In this section the relevant parts of the building regulations will be highlighted. Since the
building is supposed to carry oﬃce load, all loads are takes from category B (oﬃce areas).
4.3.1
Loads
In the designing phase, the structure is interacted by three loads: Eigenweight, uniformly
distributed force of oﬃce load, and single force oﬃce load. The eigenweight is found using the
Designer worksheet and the oﬃce loads are found in Eurocode 1 table 6.2 (EC1991-1-1) [4].
The single force load shall be applied where it is most unfavourable for the structure. All loads
are listed in table 4.1 below.
Table 4.1: Load from EC1991-1-1 tab 6.2
Loads
Magnitude
Eigenweight SL-deck
1800 kg/m3
Eigenweight beam-slab deck
2500 kg/m3
Uniformly distributed force (qk)
3 kN/m2
Single force (Qk)
4.5 kN
Section 4
Page 7 of 38
==PAGE:16==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab
Furthermore, Eurocode 1 (EC1991-1-1) [4] gives an area reduction factor (αA). This factor
reduces qk for larger buildings with large ﬂoor areas. The reduction factor is given in equation
4.1.
αA = 5
7 · ψ0 + A0
A ≤1.0
(4.1)
where ψ0 is given in National Annex to EC1990, tab. A1.1 [6], A0 = 10 m2 and A is the
loaded area.
In principle, this area reduction factor should be used, but since the total area of the building
is not deﬁned, the factor will be set equal to 1. This is an extremely conservative assumption.
4.3.2
Load combinations
The National Annex to EC1990 also provides a table with diﬀerent load combinations that
have to be taken into account. For this project, it is only table A1.2 that is relevant, since only
structural failure is assumed. The table is shown in ﬁgure 4.1.
Figure 4.1: Load combination from National Annex to EC1990
In the National Annex it is noted that for constructions that are not loaded with geotechnical
loads only load case 1 and 2 should be considered.
The relevant load cases and loads are
marked with red in ﬁgure 4.1. The load of the decks (ρ1 & ρ2) are categorised as permanent
loads (permanent last) and the oﬃce loads (qk & Qk) are categorised as variable loads (variabel
last).
Since the uniformly load qk is larger than the single force Qk, qk is categorized as
dominant load (dominerende) and Qk is categorized as other load (øvrige). It is assumed that
Section 4
Page 8 of 38
==PAGE:17==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab
the favourable variable loads have the coeﬃcient 0. The consequence coeﬃcient KFI = 1.1
because the structure is operating in high consequence class.
4.4
Force method
The force method can be used to determine reaction forces in static indeterminate structures.
The method is described in Structural and Mechanics of Structures [3] chap. 6. The method
will brieﬂy be described below in the way it is used in this project.
The force method uses an agenda, where it splits the static indeterminate structure up into
static determinate structures. The moment distribution is then determined for all the static
systems. These moment distributions are used to determine a ’deformation’ and in the end the
reaction forces are determined using these deformations. An example of the use of the force
method is shown below.
A static indeterminate structure is split into two static determinate structures, shown in
ﬁgure 4.2
Figure 4.2: Example of force method
In situation 0 the original load is applied and in situation 1 a single force is applied. It is
good practice to set the applied force X1 equal to 1. Thereby, some steps later can be skipped.
From ﬁgure 4.2 the moment distribution for both structures are determined. The moment
distributions are named M0 and M1. Then the ’deformation’ are determined using equation
4.2.
ξ01 = ξ10 =
Z L
(M0 · M1) dL
,
ξ11 =
Z L  M 2
1

dL
(4.2)

For more situations, the deformations are determined using equation 4.3.
ξij = ξji =
Z L
(Mi · Mj) dL
,
ξjj =
Z L  M 2
j

(4.3)
With these ’deformations’ determined, the reactions forces can be determined using equation
4.4. It is assumed that the total deformation of the diﬀerent structures shall be zero.
ξ1 = ξ01 + ξ11 · X11
(4.4)
For this example, the reaction in the middle RC = X11. It is in this step, it is clever to set
X1 = 1 because otherwise X1 should be added to X11.
When more structural situations are taken into account, equation 4.4 can be set up in a
matrix. This is shown in equation 4.5 and 4.6.
ξn = ξ0n + ξ1n · X11 + ... + ξnn · Xnn

(4.5)

where X11...Xnn are equal to the reactions forces in the static indeterminate system.
In
principle, the applied forces in the situations could also be moment forces, which would cause
a rotation instead of a deformation, but this is not relevant in this project and therefore it is
neglected.
Finally, the reaction force from the originally system has to be corrected. This is shown in
equation 4.7.
RA = RA 0 −RA 1 · X11 −RA 2 · X22... −RA n · Xnn
(4.7)
4.5
Anchorage
The design anchor length lbd is determined using equation 4.8 from equation (8.4) in Eurocode
2, p. 135 [5].
lbd = α1 · α2 · α3 · α4 · α5 · lb,rqd ≥lb,min
(4.8)
Where α1 −α5 are determined using page 135 in Eurocode 2, lb,qrd is a basic anchor length
Section 4
Page 10 of 38
==PAGE:19==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab
and lb,min is a minimum anchor length if no other limitations are applied.
The minimum anchor length for steel in tension is given by lb,min > max(0.3 · lb,rqd, 10 ·
ø, 100 mm). The basic anchor length is determined using equation 4.9 from equation (8.3) in
Eurocode 2, p. 134.
lb,rqd = (ø/4) · (σsd/fbd)
(4.9)
Where ø is the diameter of the anchor, σsd is the design stress of the anchor and fbd is the
bond stress of the concrete, determined using equation (8.2) in Eurocode 2, page. 133.
The anchor length is an important parameter because the consequences of a anchor that is
pulled out of the concrete could be fatal.

Page 11 of 38
==PAGE:20==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab
5
Slabs
In this section, the results for the SL-deck are presented. As mentioned in section 3 the
SL-deck are spanning one and two spans. First, the setup is clariﬁed and the calculations
are brieﬂy presented, including the load cases. Only the results with moment transfer joints
between SL-deck and beam-slab are presented, since these give the lowest dimensioning moment
and thereby the longest possible span.
After that, the results are compared to the bearing capacity found using Abeo’s Designer
worksheet.
5.1
Slab - 1 span
The SL-deck spanning one span are placed between to beam-slabs. This is shown in ﬁgure
5.1. In this ﬁgure the applied loads are also shown.
Figure 5.1: Sketch of the SL-deck spanning one span and the applied load.
5.1.1
Calculations
First, the reaction forces are determined. Since the load is symmetrically applied each support
takes half of the total load.
The total load contains of eigenwheight (ρ1 & ρ2), uniformly
distributed oﬃce load (qk) and the single force load (Qk). The single force load is applied
where it is most unfavourable, which is in the middle of the SL-deck.
When the reactions are determined, the moment distribution along the SL-deck are deter-
mined. In the calculation sheet the moment distribution are marked as Mn cen , n = 1..6.
Every time a new force is applied, a new calculation line is made. The moment distribution for
a 15.2 m SL-deck is shown in ﬁgure 5.2. The green lines represent the beam-slab deck and the
red lines represent the SL-deck.
Section 5
Page 12 of 38
==PAGE:21==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab
Figure 5.2: Moment distribution along the SL-deck. Note that the moment is per meter width.
It should be noted that the moment is per meter width. That means that the moment should
be multiplied with 2.4 m in order to get the moment applied to each deck.
The calculations of the shear and moment force distribution is shown in appendix A.
5.1.2
Load combinations
From the moment distribution in ﬁgure 5.2, the favourable and unfavourable parts of the
structure are determined. This is done in respect to both the highest positive and negative
moment.
For this structure, the highest positive moment is in the middle of the SL-deck
and the highest negative moment is in the beam-slab over the support. The favourable and
unfavourable part of the structure are shown in ﬁgure 5.3. The unfavourable parts are marked
with a ’+’ and the favourable parts are marked with a ’−’.

Figure 5.3: Favourable and unfavourable part of the structure for both positive and negative
moment.
For the positive moment, the unfavourable part spans from one support to the other. The
parts outside the supports are favourable for the positive moment in the middle of the SL-deck.
For the negative moment, the unfavourable parts spans from the left end to the right support.
The part to the right of the right support is favourable for the negative moment over the left
support.
It should be noted that the worksheet is not set up to split the load over the beam-slabs.
Therefore, the applied loads over the beam-slabs are either favourable or unfavourable. For the
load cases described above, this means in practice that all loads applied on the structure are
unfavourable. This results in a little lower critical moment, but it is evaluated that the error is
minimal, since the part where the load is applied is only 1.2m.

5.2
Slab - 2 spans
The SL-deck spanning two spans are placed between three beam-slab decks. This is shown
in ﬁgure 5.4. In this ﬁgure the applied loads are also shown.
Figure 5.4: Sketch of the SL-deck spanning two spans and the applied load.
5.2.1
Calculations
For this structure, the reaction forces are harder to determine, since the structure is a static
indeterminate structure. Therefore, the force method is used. The structure is split up into
two systems as it was showed in the example at ﬁgure 4.2. In the ﬁrst system, all loads are
applied (ρ, qk and Qk) and the reaction forces are determined by taking the moment in each
Section 5
Page 15 of 38
==PAGE:24==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab
end. For the second system, the reaction forces are equal, since the structure is symmetrically
loaded.
The single force load, Qk, should once again be placed where it is most unfavourable. In
order to avoid too complex worksheet, the single force load has once again been placed in the
middle of the SL-deck slab. It is known that the most critical point is a little closer to the
middle beam-slab, but it is evaluated that this error has a minor impact on the critical moment
and shear force.
When the reaction forces are determined, the procedure for the force method described in
section 4.4 are used. The moment distribution for situation 0 and 1 are determined followed by
the deformations using equation 4.2. By using equation 4.4, the reaction force in the middle is
determined and after that the reaction forces in the ends is corrected using equation 4.7. All
the calculations is shown in appendix B.
When all reactions are determined, the moment distribution for the structure can be deter-
mined. The moment distribution for the 2-span SL-deck spanning 15.4 m is shown in ﬁgure
5.5.
Figure 5.5: Moment distribution along the SL-deck. Note that the moment is per meter width.
Once again it should be noted that the moment force is per meter width.
5.2.2
Load combinations
From the moment distribution in ﬁgure 5.5, the favourable and unfavourable part of the
structure are determined. This is done in respect to both the highest positive and negative
Section 5
Page 16 of 38
==PAGE:25==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab
moments. Once again the highest positive moment is in the middle of the SL-deck and the
highest negative moment is in the beam-slab over the middle support. The favourable and
unfavourable part of the structure are shown in ﬁgure 5.6.
Figure 5.6: Favourable and unfavourable part of the structure for both positive and negative
moment.
For the positive moment, the unfavourable part spans from the left support to the middle
support and then again the part to the right of the right support. The part to the left of the
left support and the parts in between the middle and the right support works favourable to the
maximum positive moment in the SL-deck in between the left and the middle support.
For the negative moment, the unfavourable parts spans from the left support to the right
support.
The parts outside the left and the right supports are favourable for the negative
moment over the middle support.
Once again, it should be noted, that the work sheet is not set up to split the load over
the beam-slabs.
Therefore, the applied loads over the beam-slabs are either favourable or
unfavourable. On ﬁgure 5.6 the modiﬁed load cases for the 2 span structure are shown below
the theoretical correct. This results in a little lower moment, but it is considered that the error
is minimal, since the length of the parts where the load is applied is only 1.2 m.

5.3
Designer
The Designer worksheet from Abeo has been used to determine the highest bearing capacity
of the complex SL-deck.
As mentioned in section 3, the design of the SL-deck should ﬁt
the existing casting facilities. Therefore, the amount of pre-stressed wires in the bottom is
limited. In order to get the highest bearing capacity as possible, the SL-deck has been designed
with maximum amount of reinforcement in the bottom. The SL-deck is also designed with
pre-stressed reinforcement in the top to obtain the negative moment created in the 2-span
construction. The SL-deck is investigated with both 4 and 6 light concrete blocks in the cross
section. A cross section of the SL-deck is shown in ﬁgure 5.7 and the input parameters from
the Designer worksheet are shown in table 5.3.

are removed. For both designs, the ends of the slabs contain no blocks in order to get space
to transfer forces in the joints. The length with no blocks is set to 400 mm in the designer
worksheet.

The higher negative moment bearing capacity in the end of the SL-deck is caused by the
zone with no blocks as mentioned earlier.
It is seen in table 5.4 that long spans of 15.2 m for 1-span and 15.4 m for 2-spans create larger
forces than the SL-deck can carry. For the 1-span deck the positive moment of 1038 kNm is
larger than the bearing capacity of 861 kNm. If the span is reduced by 2 meters the positive
moment is reduced to 813.71 kNm and then the bearing capacity of the SL-deck is high enough.
Also the negative moment capacity and shear force capacity is high enough for the 13.2 m span.
For the 2-spans SL-deck the negative moment of 726.5 kNm is larger than the bearing
capacity of 666 kNm. But a further investigation shows that the negative moment in the SL-
deck is reduced to 666 kNm just 0.2 m into the SL-deck. In this zone, the negative bearing
capacity is 1016 kNm so the SL-deck actually have the capacity to carry the load of from the
15.4 m SL-deck.
The bearing capacity for the SL-deck is close to the shear forces obtained from the load cases.
The 1-span SL-deck has no problems with the shear force, but the 2-span SL-deck creates a
little larger shear force than the SL-deck with 6 block can carry. If the SL-deck is designed
with 4 block instead of 6 blocks, the bearing capacity is raised to 278 kN which is higher than
the shear force of 268 kN in the 2-span SL-deck.
It is important to remember that the forces from the load cases are conservatively high, due
to the fact that the area reduction factor is neglected. By including this factor the applied load
would be way lower and it is highly plausible that the length of the SL-decks could be a little
longer.

Integrated beam-slab
6
Beam-slab
In this section, the results for the beam-slab decks are presented. As mentioned in section
3, the beam slab is built up after the principle of a Gerber structure. To limit the amount
of variables only one solution for the beam-slab dimensions is investigated. A ﬁgure of the
beam-slab is shown in ﬁgure 6.1.
Figure 6.1: Sketch of the beam-slab Gerber structure and the applied load.
The beam-slab is designed so it ﬁts the common building dimensions i Denmark. A lot of
buildings are designed with a section width of 3 m, which is an often used width of an employee’s
oﬃce. Therefore, the beam-slab is designed with n · 3 m , n ∈N meters between the columns.
6.1
Calculations
The beam-slab is a static indeterminate structure and therefore the force method is used
as described in section 4.4. All reaction forces are determined independently (no symmetry
is used), which create 4 situations beside situation 0. The speciﬁc calculations is shown in
appendix C and D . The loads acting on the beam-slab are ρ2, qk and pSL. pSL is the weight
from the SL-deck and vary from diﬀerent spans. pSL includes the load as it is shown in ﬁgure
5.1 earlier. The single force load Qk is neglected on the beam-slab, because it is acting on the
SL-deck. The single force is acting on each SL-deck, which means there is a single force load
for every 2.4 m along the beam-slab. This setup is evaluated to be relatively conservative, and
therefore the single force load is neglected in the beam-slab. The loads are also shown at ﬁgure
6.1.
The moment distribution of the beam-slab is shown in ﬁgure 6.2
Section 6
Page 21 of 38
==PAGE:30==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab
Figure 6.2: Moment distribution along the beam-slab.
Once again it should be noted that the moment force is per meter. The total moment force
for the beam-slab is presented i table 6.2 and 6.5.
6.2
Load combinations
From the moment distribution in ﬁgure 6.2, the favourable and unfavourable parts of the
structure are determined. This is done in respect to both the highest positive and negative
moment. For this structure the highest positive moment is in the middle of the Gerber-deck
and the highest negative moment is over the second support from the facade. The favourable
and unfavourable parts of the structure are shown in ﬁgure 6.3.
Section 6
Page 22 of 38

moment.
The unfavourable parts are marked with a ’+’ and the favourable parts are marked with
a ’−’. Besides from the earlier load cases, the worksheet for the beam-slab is created so the
favourable and unfavourable parts can be applied correctly.
The favourable and unfavourable parts also include the loads on the SL-deck, so ρ1, qk, and
Qk are also weighted as favourable and unfavourable.
In the following, the results for the beam-slab for one and two SL-deck spans will be shown.

of load is taken from Teknisk St˚abi, page 108 [1]. The calculations are presented in appendix

6.5
Designer
The Designer worksheet from Abeo has also been used to determine the highest bearing
capacity of the complex beam-slab deck. As mentioned in section 3, the design of the beam-
slab deck should ﬁt the existing casting facilities like the SL-deck. Therefore, the amount of
pre-stressed wires in the bottom is limited. In order to get the highest bearing capacity as
possible, the beam-slab deck has been designed with maximum amount of reinforcement in the
bottom. The beam-slab deck is also designed with pre-stressed reinforcement in the top, to
obtain the higher negative moment created in the 2-span construction. The beam-slab deck is
investigated with zero light concrete blocks in the cross section, because the forces to absorb
are large. A cross section of the beam-slab deck is shown in ﬁgure 6.4 and the input parameters
from the Designer worksheet are shown in table 6.6.
Figure 6.4: Cross section of the beam-slab deck slab.

forces in the joints.

and the largest shear forces along the beam-slab are 412 kN for 1 SL-deck and 888 kN for 2
SL-decks. By taking the neglection of the area reduction parameter into consideration, it is
reasonable that the beam-slab with 1 SL-deck can absorb the shear forces. The beam-slab with
2 SL-decks is evaluated to require more transverse reinforcement.
Since the bearing capacity of the transversal reinforcement is used to the limit, the bearing
capacity in the cross section is problematic. Since the reinforcement can only be used once,
there is minimal bearing capacity available for the cross section. The big problem is the negative
moment created by the 2 SL-decks. The negative moment created is 1071 kNm which is a little
Section 6
Page 28 of 38
==PAGE:37==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab
more than the beam-slab can absorb along the slab. A serious amount of reinforcement has to
be placed across in the top of the beam-slab to make the bearing capacity of the beam-slab
high enough.
The shear forces from the load going across the beam-slab is not the biggest problem, but as
mentioned before, the shear capacity can only be used once. With a little more reinforcement
and the inclusion of the area reduction parameter, the bearing capacity for the 1 SL-deck span
could be high enough.
Several actions could be made to the structure. First, the length of the SL-deck span could
be reduced. This would result in lower forces going across the beam-slab, both the shear force
and moment force. Also the moment force and shear force going along the beam-slab would be
reduced.
Another action could be more supports under the beam-slab. By including more supports,
the length of the spans along the beam-slab would be reduced and thereby the shear forces over
the supports as well.
These two solutions would make the area of the oﬃce smaller and less ﬂexible, which goes
against the idea of creating an open oﬃce with long deck spans and few columns. This leads to
a third action, which is to put more reinforcement in the beam-slabs. This action would make
the production procedure more complex, but it might make the idea of an open oﬃce area
possible. Otherwise the height of the decks could be increased. This would lead to a higher
bearing capacity, but also a higher eigenweight.
Section 6
Page 29 of 38
==PAGE:38==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab
7
Joints
To connect the SL-deck to the beam-slab deck and make the connection able to transfer
moment forces, a joint with anchors has to be created. Like before, the design should ﬁt the
existing casting facilities, which is a challenge when the deck are pre-stressed.
To implement the anchors in the joints, the joints have to be cast on the construction site.
To get the maximum bearing capacity in the joint, the full cross section should be ﬁlled with
concrete on the construction site in order to make maximum forcer transfer between the decks.
First the design is presented.
7.1
Design
The design of the joint is shown in ﬁgure 7.1.
Figure 7.1: Joint design. SL-deck - cast mortar - Beam-slab deck
In the bottom of the SL-deck there is a gap between the SL-deck and beam-slab deck spanning
the full width of the SL-deck. This area has to be ﬁlled with concrete, and since the gravity
pulls down, the concrete has to come from the top of the deck. Therefore, some holes are made.
There is placed 6 holes in total in each SL-deck and they are placed in extension of the light
concrete blocks. As mentioned earlier, there is no light concrete blocks in the end but the holes
are placed over where the light concrete blocks would have been, to avoid too much exposure
of the pre-stressed concrete. The volume that is cast in the construction site is shown in ﬁgure
7.2.
Section 7
Page 30 of 38
==PAGE:39==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab
Figure 7.2: The volume/area cast on the construction site to connect SL-decks and beam-slab
decks.
On ﬁgure 7.2 it is seen that the gap in the bottom is tight. A PhD student on DTU, Mia
Schou Møller Lund, has investigated some similar connections between SL-decks and she has
recommended a mortar called SikeGrout 210. This mortar has a compression strength way
higher than the concrete and the mortar is extremely plastic so it can easily ﬁll the gap.
Due to the plastic mortar, the gaps have to be sealed. This is not a big problem, since
the SL-deck slabs and beam-slabs have to be supported under construction in order to avoid
unpredicted moment forces. The Manufacture Meva produces form work made by strong plastic
instead of wood. This product has several advantages. First, Meva’s form work is durable and
they guarantee up to 7 years of lifetime. Beside that, holes can be drilled in the form work if
needed. After the form work is removed, the hole can be ﬁlled out with wall putty and the
form work is ready to be used again. The surface of the cast material remains plain instead of
getting a wood look when wood is used as form work.
On ﬁgure 7.2 it is seen that the mortar has an extension in the top into both the SL-deck and
beam-slab. This is created so the SL-deck and beam-slab can be anchored together when the
gap is ﬁlled. The dimensions of these holes are 110 mm wide, 400 mm long into each slab and
60 mm deep. Unfortunately, the anchors have to pass under the reinforcement in the top of
the beam-slab, but as long as the deck is stored for a limited time before construction, this has
a minimal eﬀect. In appendix E, the anchor length have been calculated for three anchors of
d = 12 mm per anchor hole. The calculations have been made based on the equations presented
in section 4. The anchor length is estimated to be minimum 260 mm into the decks, so the
anchor hole should be long enough.
The moment bearing capacity of the joint is calculated in appendix F. The anchors are
calculated to deliver a negative moment bearing capacity of 256 kNm. This is only enough to
carry a SL-deck of 9.4 m in the 2-span system. For the 1-span system a positive moment is
created in the joint. Also the beam-slabs create a positive moment in the joints, but these a
slightly small. Unfortunately, this has been discovered in a late phase of the project, and an
Section 7
Page 31 of 38
==PAGE:40==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab
exact solution for this has not been ﬁgured out. The big problem is, that it is hard to make
anchors in the bottom of the joint. A solution could be to make a hook, where some curved
reinforcement goes in between a reinforcement hanger, and when the mortal ﬁlls around, the
hook should be able to take positive moment forces. This should be investigated in further
design.
Figure 7.3: The end of the SL-deck slab.
On ﬁgure 7.3 a larger model of the SL-deck joint is shown. It is seen that the pre-stressed
reinforcement in the bottom goes through the gap. This reinforcement is cut of just after the
slab is removed from the casting facility. One of the issues is to make a form that can create
the hole for the gap and let the pre-stressed reinforcement pass through while the slab is cast.
One solution is a metal form with holes. Since the reinforcement in the bottom always is in
the same place and has the same size, only one form should be used. The metal is good for
mass production because the durability is high. The joint between the metal form and the
reinforcement could be a rubber ring, which would keep the concrete out of the gab.
Section 7
Page 32 of 38
==PAGE:41==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab
Figure 7.4: The side of the beam-slab
On ﬁgure 7.4 a larger model of the side of the beam-slab is shown. The shelf in the bottom
can easily be made by a metal form. The anchor hole around the reinforcement in the top is
a bigger problem. Several approaches can be made to solve this problem. One of these is to
use a material, where the reinforcement is pulled through. When the concrete is hardened, the
material is destroyed and removed. In the end, this could be an expensive solution, because the
form cannot be reused. Another approach could be a metal form that can ﬂip up in between
the reinforcement. The idea is that after hardening, the sides of the form can ﬂip down and
the form can be bulled out under the reinforcement and reused.
It is assumed, that the shear force reinforcement in the end is ﬁtted to the new shape of the
joint, so the shear force bearing capacity is the same as calculated in the Designer Worksheet.
Therefore, a further investigation of the shear bearing capacity of the joint has not been made.
Section 7
Page 33 of 38
==PAGE:42==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab - Conclusion
8
Conclusion
Through the investigation of the SL-decks, beam-slab decks and joints, some observations
have been made.
First, the moment bearing capacity of a SL-deck is calculated to be 861/ −666 kNm
(−1016 kNm in the end).
This is enough to carry a 13.2 m SL-deck in a 1-span system
or a 15.4 m SL-deck in a 2-span system. The shear force bearing capacity is 231 kN, which
reduces the length of the 2-span SL-deck to 13.4 m. If the SL-deck is designed with 4 light
concrete blocks instead of 6, the shear force bearing capacity is raised to 278 kNm which is
enough to carry the 15.4 m SL-deck in the 2-span structure.
The long length of SL-deck create challenges with the beam-slabs. Due to the long length
of the SL-decks, a huge amount of weight are applied to the beam-slabs. The length of the
spans in the beam-slab is relatively short, and therefore the moment forces are not that big.
On the other hand, the large amount of load create huge shear forces over the supports. For the
designed beam-slab with 0 light concrete blocks, the shear force bearing capacity is calculated
to 353 kN. For the 1-span structure, the shear force along the beam-slab is 371 kN for a
SL-deck with the length of 13.2 m. For the 2-span beam-slab in the middle, the shear force
along the beam-slab is 888 kN for a SL-deck with the length of 15.4 m. Beside that, shear
forces are also applied across the beam-slab. This means that the length of the SL-decks have
to be reduced.
For all the applied loads it should be noted that an area reduction coeﬃcient have been
neglected, because the area of the oﬃce building is unknown. When the speciﬁc area is deter-
mined, the oﬃce load qk can be reduced and the forces in the slabs will thereby also be reduced.
A solution for the joint has also been investigated. The form work on the concrete factory
can be made out of metal and can thereby be reused. The form work allows the pre-stressed
reinforcement to pass by. When the form work is removed a little gap is created, so the SL-decks
and beam-slabs can be connected on the construction site using a strong plastic mortar. The
negative moment bearing capacity of the anchors in the joint is calculated to 256 kNm. This is
only enough to carry a SL-deck with the length of 9.4 m in a 2-span structure. For the 1-span
structure the moment in the joint are positive, which the joint has not been designed to.
Section 8
Page 34 of 38
==PAGE:43==
DTU - Byg
Integrated beam-slab - Conclusion
8.1
Comments on limitations
One parameter that would change the results is the inclusion of the area reduction parameter.
Another parameter is diﬀerent dimensions of the building. Shorter SL-deck and maybe more
supports under the beam-slab could reduce the high shear forces in the beam-slab.
8.2
Perspective
In a further investigation of the bearing capacity of the beam-slabs, the torsion force should
be included. The Designer worksheet is not programmed to calculate on the torsional forces.
For a beam-slab with a 15.4 m long SL-deck at one side, it is predictable that the torsional
forces might be large.
